      TSI-B ENCH : B ENCHMARKING T IME S ERIES I MPUTATION
002
003    Anonymous authors
004    Paper under double-blind review

                                                       A BSTRACT
009
               Effective imputation is a crucial preprocessing step for time series analysis. Despite the
010
               development of numerous deep learning algorithms for time series imputation, the commu-
011
               nity lacks standardized and comprehensive benchmark platforms to effectively evaluate
012            imputation performance across different settings. Moreover, although many deep learning
013            forecasting algorithms have demonstrated excellent performance, whether their modelling
014            achievements can be transferred to time series imputation tasks remains unexplored. To
015            bridge these gaps, we develop TSI-Bench, the first (to our knowledge) comprehensive bench-
016            mark suite for time series imputation utilizing deep learning techniques. The TSI-Bench
017            pipeline standardizes experimental settings to enable fair evaluation of imputation algo-
018            rithms and identification of meaningful insights into the influence of domain-appropriate
019            missing rates and patterns on model performance. Furthermore, TSI-Bench innovatively
020
               provides a systematic paradigm to tailor time series forecasting algorithms for imputation
               purposes. Our extensive study across 34,804 experiments, 28 algorithms, and 8 datasets
021
               with diverse missingness scenarios demonstrates TSI-Bench’s effectiveness in diverse down-
022
               stream tasks and potential to unlock future directions in time series imputation research
023            and analysis. All source code and experiment logs are released.
024
025

043   The current research landscape in time series imputation is marked by inconsistencies, knowledge gaps,
044   and wide performance disparities reported in literature reviews (Fang & Wang, 2020; Wang et al., 2024).
045   These variations arise from differences in data characteristics and dimensionalities, domain-specific missing
046   patterns, and task-specific requirements. They are further amplified by the heterogeneity of implementation,

059   experiment designs, and evaluation settings. This complicates model and study comparisons, significantly
060   influencing algorithm performance, but remains deeply under-explored (Qian et al., 2024).
061
062
      To bridge existing gaps, we designed the first (to the best of our knowledge) comprehensive time series
      imputation benchmark, named TSI-Bench. TSI-Bench is built on top of an in-house developed imputation
063
      suite, which includes 28 high-performing time series imputation and forecasting algorithms. It enables
064
      efficient and fair evaluation of newly proposed methods against existing baselines through a standardized
065   ecosystem. This ecosystem provides a comprehensive set of data preprocessing tools, missingness simulation
066   techniques, and performance evaluation methods. Researchers can use these resources to tailor the imputation
067   task to suit the specific needs of their datasets, application domains, and downstream tasks. Notably, because
068   forecasting is intertwined with imputation, TSI-Bench also incorporates a systematic paradigm to leverage
069   time series forecasting models for imputation purposes. A comparison of TSI-Bench with prior works is
070   presented in Table 1.
071   The primary contributions of TSI-Bench are as follows.
072
073   • The first comprehensive benchmark for evaluating time series imputation, which includes 28 ready-to-
074     use algorithms spanning both imputation-based and forecasting-based approaches. The benchmark utilizes
075     eight datasets across four diverse domains (air quality, traffic, electricity, and healthcare) with various
076     dimensionalities, missing patterns, and data types.
077   • TSI-Bench offers both research- and application-driven benchmarking perspectives, enabling a stan-
078     dardized analysis of four critical dimensions of the imputation process, identified through the study of
079     various domain requirements: (1) the capacity to simulate varying degrees of missingness and assess its im-
080     pact on model performance; (2) the flexibility to incorporate different missing patterns (e.g., subsequence or
081     block missingness) in time series, tailored to domain-specific needs, in addition to single-point missingness;
082     (3) the selection of imputation or forecasting models; and (4) the alignment with expected downstream
083
        tasks. Researchers dealing with missing data can leverage TSI-Bench to evaluate the suitability of different
        imputation approaches for their specific problem, dataset, and downstream applications.
084
085   • Identifying and addressing limitations inherent in existing TSI evaluation schemes to ensure rigorous
086     and equitable comparisons. We build an open-source ecosystem encompassing a standardized data pre-
087
        processing pipeline, flexible and user-driven missingness simulation, metric utilization, and hyperparameter
        tuning. This ecosystem not only facilitates the effortless reproduction of our results, but also provides users
088
        with the tools to assess and integrate their datasets and models into our ecosystem with ease.
089
090   Our collective experimental efforts with TSI-Bench assess the efficacy of the different algorithms in a
091   total of 34,804 experiments. Our extensive experiments reveal the following key findings: (1) Different
092   missing patterns and rates significantly influence the performance of imputation methods, and none of the 28
093   algorithms significantly outperforms the others across all settings, emphasizing the importance of the model

                                                            2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


094
      tuning and data processing capabilities enabled by TSI-Bench; (2) Forecasting architectures demonstrate
095
      effectiveness when used as an imputation backbone; (3) Imputation enhances both flexibility and effectiveness
096   across downstream tasks by enabling the broad application of high-quality imputed time series.
097
098
099   2    P RELIMINARIES AND R ELATED W ORK
100
101   Notation A time series collection of length T can be represented by a matrix X = (x1 , . . . , xt , . . . , xT )⊤ ∈
102   RT ×D , with D being the data dimension. An observation xt ∈ R1×D is acquired at time step t. To encode
103   the presence of missing values in X, we introduce a mask matrix M ∈ RT ×D , i.e., Mt,d = 1 if Xt,d exists,
104   otherwise Mt,d = 0. We denote the ground truth by X̃ ∈ RT ×D . Given a reconstructed time series X̄ by an
105   imputer, the objective of time series imputation is to obtain the imputed time series:
106
107                                           X̂ = M ⊙ X + (1 − M) ⊙ X̄,                                             (1)
108
      so as to minimize the discrepancy between the imputed X̂ and the ground truth X̃ as possible. ⊙ denotes the
109
      element-wise multiplication.
110
111
112
      Related Work - Imputation Methods Deep imputation methods have recently gained popularity in
      handling missing values in time series data due to their ability to model the nonlinearities and temporal
113
      patterns inherent in time series. The literature contains a wide range of deep imputation methods, which can
114
      be broadly classified as either predictive or generative. Predictive imputation methods aim to consistently
115   predict deterministic values for missing components within the time series (Yoon et al., 2019; Wu et al.,
116   2023; Cini et al., 2022; Marisca et al., 2022; Ma et al., 2019; Bansal et al., 2021). Existing models employ
117   a variety of neural network architectures, including Recurrent Neural Networks (RNNs), Convolutional
118   Neural Networks (CNNs), Graph Neural Network (GNN), and attention mechanisms. Highly cited examples
119   include GRU-D (Che et al., 2018a) and BRITS (Cao et al., 2018), which achieve improved imputation
120   performance by integrating a time-decay mechanism into RNNs to capture irregularities caused by missing
121   values. TimesNet (Wu et al., 2023) is a highly-performing CNN model that incorporates a Fast Fourier
122   Transformation to restructure 1D time series into a 2D format. Finally, SAITS (Du et al., 2023) is an
123
      attention-based model comprising two diagonal-masked self-attention blocks and a weighted-combination
      block, which leverages attention weights and missingness indicators to enhance imputation precision.
124
125   Generative deep imputation models, built upon generative models such as VAEs, GANs, and diffusion models,
126   are distinguished by their ability to generate varied outputs for missing observations, reflecting the inherent
127   uncertainty of the imputation process (Mulyadi et al., 2021; Kim et al., 2023; Luo et al., 2018; 2019; Alcaraz
128   & Strodthoff, 2023; Chen et al., 2023b; Yang et al., 2024). Examples include GP-VAE (Fortuin et al., 2020),
129
      which exploits a Gaussian process as a prior distribution to model temporal dependencies in the incomplete
      time series; US-GAN (Miao et al., 2021b) increases the complexity of discriminator training by compromising
130
      the masking matrix, subsequently leading to the improvement of generator performance through adversarial
131
      dynamics; and finally CSDI (Tashiro et al., 2021) adapts diffusion models for TSI through a conditioned
132   training strategy by employing a subset of the observed data as conditional inputs to guide the generation
133   process of the missing segments within the time series.
134
135
      Related Work - Forecasting Frameworks for Imputation In recent years, numerous deep learning archi-
136   tectures have exhibited exceptional performance in time series forecasting problems. Such as Transformer-
137   based structures (Wu et al., 2021; Zhou et al., 2021a; Nie et al., 2023), GNN-based structures (Cao et al.,
138   2020; Wu et al., 2020; Liu et al., 2022b; Jin et al., 2023), MLP-based structures (Ekambaram et al., 2023;
139   Chen et al., 2023a; Wang et al., 2023a), and diffusion-based structures (Rasul et al., 2021; Tashiro et al., 2021;
140   Shen & Kwok, 2023; Shen et al., 2024).

155
156   Transformers excel at time series forecasting due to their ability to model long-range dependencies and
157   capture complex temporal patterns (Wen et al., 2023). Examples include Informer (Zhou et al., 2021b) and
158   Crossformer (Zhang & Yan, 2022), which utilize different attention mechanisms to weigh the importance
159
      of different time steps dynamically. This is particularly beneficial for time series data, which often contains
      long-term dependencies and intricate patterns. At the same time, researchers showed that simple models can
160
      sometimes outperform Transformer architectures in long-term time series forecasting tasks (Zeng et al., 2023).
161
      MLP structures, known for their lower complexity and computational efficiency, have attracted growing
162   interest from researchers. For instance, TSMixer (Chen et al., 2023a) extends the MLP mixer concept from
163   computer vision (Tolstikhin et al., 2021) to time series forecasting by performing mixing operations along
164   both time and feature dimensions (Ekambaram et al., 2023). Other innovative MLP-based designs, such
165   as Koopa (Liu et al., 2023c) and FITS (Xu et al., 2023), further highlight the promising potential of MLP
166   approaches in advancing time series forecasting.
167
      The progress in forecasting models has the potential to be beneficial in imputation problems. Essentially,
168   forecasting and imputation problems can be regarded as two similar tasks in time series analysis, as both
169   require obtaining temporal representations of existing time series data to predict future values (Wen et al.,
170   2019) or to impute missing values (Wang et al., 2024; 2023b). Given that time series forecasting is a highly
171   active research area within time series analysis, a framework that enables researchers to directly apply
172   forecasting methods to imputation challenges could potentially lead to the development of more efficient
173   imputation techniques. This, in turn, might further advance research in time series analysis.
174
175
      3       T HE S ETUP OF TSI-B ENCH
176
177
      In this section, we introduce the overview of the models evaluated in the experiments, along with the dataset
178
      descriptions and pertinent implementation details.
179
180
181
      3.1       TSI-B ENCH S UITE
182
      TSI-Bench is a standardized suite that streamlines the imputation process and downstream tasks, built upon
183   the PyPOTS ecosystem (Du, 2023). PyPOTS provides easy, standardized access to a variety of algorithms for
184   imputation, classification, clustering, and forecasting. It supports the entire imputation workflow, from data
185   loading and processing to model building and data imputation. The TSI-Bench workflow, depicted in Figure 2,
186   begins with data being loaded from the time series database, TSDB, which serves as the data warehouse. The
187   loaded data is then transformed into partially observed time series (POTS) using PyGrinder, a flexible,

                                                                                     4
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


188
      user-driven missingness simulation tool. The POTS is subsequently processed by BenchPOTS, which
189
      provides standardized data preprocessing pipelines to benchmark machine learning algorithms on POTS.
190   Following preprocessing, the time series is passed through an imputation model implemented in PyPOTS
191   to estimate the missing values, resulting in the imputed time series. Finally, the imputed data is utilised by
192   downstream models for further analysis, such as classification, regression, and forecasting.
193
194   3.2   DATASETS
195
196   In TSI-Bench, we conduct experiments using eight diverse and representative datasets from four domains:
197   air quality, traffic, electricity, and healthcare. In the air quality domain, the BeijingAir dataset contains
198   hourly data on six pollutants and meteorological variables from 12 sites in Beijing (Zhang et al., 2017). The
199   ItalyAir dataset includes 9,358 hourly records of chemical sensor responses and pollutant concentrations
200
      collected from March 2004 to February 2005 (De Vito et al., 2008). In the traffic domain, the PeMS dataset
      provides hourly road occupancy rates from the San Francisco Bay Area freeways, while the Pedestrian
201
      dataset consists of automated pedestrian counts in Melbourne for the year 2017 (Tang et al., 2020). In the
202
      electricity domain, the Electricity dataset records hourly electricity consumption for 370 clients, and the ETT
203   dataset includes power load and oil temperature data, with the ETT_h1 subset used for our experiments (Zhou
204   et al., 2021a). For healthcare, the PhysioNet2012 dataset comprises 12,000 Intensive Care Unit (ICU) patient
205   records, focusing on in-hospital mortality prediction in the presence of 80% missing values (Silva et al., 2012).
206   Additionally, the PhysioNet2019 dataset includes clinical data from 40,336 ICU patients, using subset A for
207   analysis (Reyna et al., 2020).
208
209   3.3   M ODELS
210
211   To comprehensively evaluate the performance of various models in the time series imputation task, we
212   utilise 28 different models integrated into the PyPOTS Ecosystem. These models are based on distinct
213   architectures, originally designed for different tasks. Among the evaluated models are Transformer ar-
214
      chitectures, including Transformer (Yıldız et al., 2022), Pyraformer (Liu et al., 2021), Autoformer (Wu
      et al., 2021), Informer (Zhou et al., 2021b), Crossformer (Zhang & Yan, 2022), PatchTST (Nie et al., 2023),
215
      ETSformer (Woo et al., 2022), Nonstationary Transformer (Liu et al., 2022c), SAITS (Du et al., 2023), and
216
      iTransformer (Liu et al., 2023b); RNN architectures, such as MRNN (Yoon et al., 2018), BRITS (Cao
217   et al., 2018), and GRUD (Che et al., 2018b); CNN architectures, including MICN (Wang et al., 2022),
218   SCINet (Liu et al., 2022a), and TimesNet (Wu et al., 2023); GNN architectures, such as StemGNN (Cao
219   et al., 2020); MLP architectures, such as FiLM (Zhou et al., 2022), DLinear (Zeng et al., 2023), Koopa (Liu
220   et al., 2024), and FreTS (Yi et al., 2024); and Generative architectures, including GP-VAE (Fortuin et al.,
221   2020), US-GAN (Miao et al., 2021a), and CSDI (Tashiro et al., 2021), which are based on VAE, GAN,
222   and diffusion models. Additionally, we apply several traditional methods, such as Mean, Median, LOCF
223   (Last Observation Carried Forward), and Linear interpolation. For the original forecasting models (e.g.,
224   Transformer, Pyraformer, Autoformer), we adapt the embedding strategies and training methodologies from
225
      SAITS (Du et al., 2023) to reimplement them as imputation methods. Further details on the benchmarked
      models and the systematic approach used to adapt time series forecasting models for imputation purposes can
226
      be found in Appendix B and Appendix C.5.
227
228
      3.4   I MPLEMENTATION D ETAILS
229
230   Dataset Preprocessing: Except PhysioNet2012, PhysioNet2019, and Pedestrian, which already contain
231   separated time series samples, all other datasets are split into training, validation, and test sets according to the
232   time period. A sliding window function is then applied to generate individual data samples. Standardization is
233   performed on all datasets. For further details on dataset preprocessing, please refer to Appendix A.2. Missing
234   Patterns: To more effectively simulate missing scenarios in time series, we deviate from the traditional

257   categories of missing mechanisms introduced by (Rubin, 1976). Instead, we implement point missing,
258   subsequence missing, and block missing, which are particularly representative of common missing data
259   situations in time series (Mitra et al., 2023; Cini et al., 2022; Khayati et al., 2020). These patterns capture
260   both random and structured missingness, reflecting the dynamic nature of time series data and providing a
261   robust framework for evaluating imputation strategies. For a detailed discussion and visual representation of
262   these missing patterns, please refer to Appendix C.1. Evaluation Metrics: To assess imputation performance,
263
      we use mean absolute error (MAE), mean squared error (MSE), and mean relative error (MRE) (Cao et al.,
      2018). The mathematical formulations for these metrics can be found in Appendix C.2. Additionally, we
264
      collect inference time and the number of trainable parameters for further discussion. Hyper-parameter
265
      Optimisation: To ensure fair comparisons, hyper-parameter optimisation is applied to all deep-learning-based
266   imputation algorithms. This process is implemented using PyPOTS (Du, 2023) and NNI (Microsoft), with
267   details provided in Appendix C.3. Downstream Task Design: To evaluate the impact of imputation on
268   downstream analysis, we perform various tasks using XGBoost, RNN, and Transformer models on the
269   imputed datasets. Given that XGBoost has an inherent capability to manage incomplete data, we set XGBoost
270   without imputation as the baseline for comparison. Further details on the downstream task design can be
271   found in Appendix C.4.
272
273
      4   E XPERIMENTAL ANALYSIS
274
275
      We conduct a comprehensive analysis involving up to 34,804 experiments across 28 algorithms and 8 diverse
276
      domain datasets. The experiments are structured to examine three key perspectives: (1) Data Perspective:
277   We evaluate different missing rates (10%, 50%, and 90%) and missing patterns (point, subsequence, block)
278   across all datasets. (2) Model Perspective: We analyze each method based on its target task type (forecasting,
279   imputation) and architecture (Transformer/attention, RNN, CNN, MLP, etc.), while also assessing the
280   influence of model size and inference time on practical applications. (3) Downstream Task Perspective: We
281   assess the quality of the imputed data using three architectures (XGBoost, RNN, Transformer) across three

                                                                            6
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


282
283
284
285
286
287
288
289               Figure 3: Performance of 50% block missing (left) and 50% subseq missing (right).
290
291   classical time series downstream tasks (classification, regression, forecasting) to highlight the importance and
292   impact of imputation on downstream performance. Full results are available in Appendix D.
293
294   4.1   DATA P ERSPECTIVE
295
296   We conduct experiments on the following aspects of data and missing patterns: datasets from different
297
      application domains, missing rates, and different missing patterns.
298   Application Domains Table 2 shows that different models show significant performance disparities across
299   datasets from different application domains. For instance, Autoformer performs well on the Pedestrian dataset
300   (with an MAE of 0.197) but performs poorly on the ItalyAir dataset (with an MAE of 0.295). This trend is
301   also observed within the same application domain. BRITS excels on the BeijingAir dataset (with an MAE
302   of 0.127) but shows worse performance on the ItalyAir dataset (with an MAE of 0.235). Overall, different
303
      models demonstrate varying performance across different application domains and datasets, with each model
      having its own strengths and weaknesses in specific datasets. Even in simpler application fields, such as
304
      electricity, we can get better results using traditional LOCF and Linear methods.
305
306   Missing Rates As the missingness data rate increases from 10% to 90%, there is a general trend of performance
307   degradation across all models and datasets, as shown in Appendix D. For instance, Autoformer’s MAE on the
308   BeijingAir dataset rises from 0.257 at 10% to 0.898 at 50% missingness. Different models exhibit varying
309
      sensitivity to missing data, with BRITS maintaining relatively lower errors compared to others, whereas
      CSDI shows extreme sensitivity. CSDI’s MAE on the ItalyAir dataset jumps from 0.539 at 10% missingness
310
      to 4.492 at 90%. CSDI is a conditional diffusion model that performs poorly and becomes less stable with
311
      high missing rates primarily due to noise accumulation and lack of contextual information. Computational
312   time also varies, with CSDI showing substantially longer times at higher missing rates, while models like
313   Autoformer and DLinear remain consistent. We want to highlight the importance of selecting appropriate
314   models based on the expected missing data rate. For models with similar performance, it is better to consider
315   computational efficiency for practical implementation.
316
      Missing Patterns It is evident that different patterns significantly impact model performance, as shown in
317
      Figure 3. In general, block and subsequence missing patterns result in higher error metrics compared to point
318   missingness, indicating that continuous missing data have a greater adverse effect on model performance.
319   Specifically, point missingness has the least impact on most models, because missing data can be easily
320   interpolated from adjacent points; for example, BRITS performs excellently under this pattern. In contrast,
321   block missingness leads to more significant information loss over extended periods, making it challenging for
322   models to infer missing data from distant points, as seen with the increased MAE and MSE for Autoformer and
323   Crossformer. Subsequence missingness further degrades model performance since the missing subsequences
324   may contain critical temporal patterns or trends that the models cannot easily reconstruct. Thus, different
325   missing patterns affect model performance to varying degrees, with continuous missing patterns (block and
326
      subsequence missing) posing greater challenges.
327   We further provide visualization of imputed data from all imputation methods on ETT_h1 and Electricity
328   in Appendix D.6. As shown in Figure 9 and Figure 10, different features within the same dataset exhibit

341
342   substantial variation. Some features like Feature #76 in Electricity exhibit limited temporal dependencies,
343   whereas others like Feature #1 in ETT_h1 present clear temporal patterns, such as step changes. Moreover,
344   when comparing the same feature across different missing patterns (e.g., point versus block missingness), the
345
      imputation results can differ significantly. While most algorithms perform well with point missingness, they
      often struggle with block missingness, which demands the preservation of longer-term dependencies. The
346
      results suggest that it is challenging for any single imputation algorithm to consistently perform well across
347
      different datasets and missingness scenarios effectively.
348
349   Remark 1: We suggest choosing imputation models tailored to data characteristics, such as application
350   domains and missing patterns. Practitioners can leverage our comprehensive insights to make informed
351
      model choices to narrow their trials and experiments in selecting models, enhancing the development and
      open-source accessibility of time series imputation.
352
353
354   4.2           M ODEL P ERSPECTIVE
355
356
      Given the space constraints, we will focus our discussion in this section on the model architectures and the
      primary task assuming a 10% missing rate.
357
358   Architecture The inference time, model size, and performance metrics of different architectures shown
359   in Figure 4 and Table 2 indicate the following: Transformer/attention-based models, such as Autoformer
360   and Informer, demonstrate excellent performance, but some models like Crossformer and SAITS have
361   larger model sizes, potentially leading to lower inference efficiency. RNN-based models like BRITS show
362
      good performance (low MSE, MAE) but have longer inference times and moderate model sizes. CNN-
      based models like SCINet have faster inference times but relatively larger model sizes, with slightly lower
363
      performance compared to Transformer and RNN architectures. MLP-based models like DLinear have
364
      clear advantages in inference time and model size, with moderate performance, making them suitable for
365   applications requiring quick inference and smaller models. CSDI, as a generative model, performs well on
366   MAE, but has an extremely long inference time and moderate model size, suitable for scenarios demanding
367   very high performance. Traditional methods, due to their limited scope, usually do not yield optimal results,
368   especially in datasets with high missing rates and complexity. However, they can be more efficient and perform
369   better on simpler datasets. In summary, different architectures have their own strengths and weaknesses in
370   terms of inference time, model size, and performance metrics, necessitating the selection of an appropriate
371   model based on specific application requirements.
372   Forecasting Backbones for Imputation With the adaptation paradigm in Appendix C.5, we transfer the
373   backbone of the time series forecasting models to the imputation task and compare their performance against
374   typical imputation methods. The results are categorized in Table 2, and detailed descriptions of the backbones
375   are discussed in Appendix B. For forecasting backbones, DLinear, with the shortest inference time of 0.17

390   seconds and a model size of 204K, shows efficient performance with an MAE of 0.215 on the Beijing dataset,
391   making it ideal for real-time applications. In imputation, BRITS has an inference time of 17.04 seconds, a
392   model size of 3.5M, and performs excellently on the Beijing dataset with an MAE of 0.127. CSDI, however,
393   has a long inference time of 417.52 seconds, a model size of 244K, and higher MSE values, limiting its
394
      practical use despite advanced techniques. Generally, methods originally designed for imputation do not
      exhibit extraordinary performance compared to those designed for forecasting. In many scenarios, the adapted
395
      forecasting methods tend to perform better. A potential reason is that more research has been conducted on
396
      time series forecasting compared to time series imputation. Therefore, time series forecasting models are more
397   updated and advanced in capturing temporal information and multivariate correlations. The experimental
398   results demonstrate the success of our adaptation paradigm.
399
400
      Remark 2: Existing forecasting backbones can be effectively applied to imputation tasks, enhancing the
      interaction between different time series tasks. This suggests that the imputation field can benefit from
401
      leveraging techniques and models originally designed for forecasting and other time series tasks. This
402
      cross-application improves the versatility and efficiency of time series models, opening new avenues for
403   research and practical applications.
404
405
      4.3             D OWNSTREAM TASKS P ERSPECTIVE
406
407   Forecasting Task We evaluated the downstream forecasting performance of various models on the ETT_h1
408   dataset. As shown in Figure 5 (right), certain imputation methods significantly improve downstream forecast-
409   ing performance. For instance, the Nonstationary imputation method consistently enhances accuracy across
410   multiple models. Transformer-based models show notable gains with robust imputation strategies. XGBoost
411   without imputation results in a higher MAE, but applying Transformer models with imputation consistently
412   reduces the MAE. Overall, selecting the right imputation method is crucial for improving downstream
413   forecasting task performance.
414   Classification Task In this test, we apply different models for imputation, followed by three types of
415   classifiers. The goal is to assess the performance improvement brought by various imputation methods, as
416   shown in Figure 5 (left). For instance, iTransformer achieves a ROC_AUC of 0.771 without imputation,
417   which increases to 0.852 after imputation. The non-stationary method performs best with an ROC_AUC of
418   0.860, showcasing strong robustness in handling missing data. Overall, imputation significantly enhances
419   classification performance, and the choice of imputation method is crucial for optimizing downstream tasks.
420   Regression Task As shown in Table 3, imputation methods significantly affect downstream regression
421   performance under three missing patterns. For example, the RNN-based BRITS model achieves the lowest
422   MAE of 0.826 in the block missing and subsequence scenarios, demonstrating its robustness in handling data

448   patterns. In contrast, traditional methods such as Mean and Median imputation result in higher MAE values,
449
      underscoring their limitations. The improved performance of models like XGBoost applied to imputed data
      further emphasizes the importance of robust imputation for enhancing regression performance.
450
451   Remark 3: Imputation is not only critical on its own but also plays a key role in improving the performance of
452   downstream tasks. Additionally, pre-imputation demonstrates high adaptability across different downstream
453   tasks within the same datasets. Once high-quality imputed time series are generated, they can be effectively
454   leveraged across a wide range of downstream applications.
455
456   5   C ONCLUSION
457
458   In this paper, we introduce TSI-Bench, a comprehensive benchmark for time series imputation. Our evaluation
459   encompasses 28 algorithms across 8 real-world datasets, illustrating that successful imputation hinges on
460   factors like deep learning architectures, missing rates, missing patterns across space-time, and the datasets
461   themselves. Additionally, incorporating uncertainty quantification in the evaluation process is essential, as it
462   enables an assessment of confidence in the imputed data and helps to identify the limitations and reliability of
463
      the results. However, uncertainty quantification in time series imputation remains an emerging area, limited by
      the complexity of sequential dependencies, high-dimensional features, and the lack of standardized metrics.
464
465   Despite the complexities, TSI-Bench provides valuable insights and practical guidelines for time series
466   imputation in real-world scenarios. We envision TSI-Bench as a continually evolving project. Our future
467   plans include the integration of more advanced deep learning models, the deployment of more equitable
468   evaluation metrics, and the expansion of our dataset collection. Additionally, we are committed to improving
469
      the user-friendliness of TSI-Bench, aiming to establish it as the standard tool for time series imputation.

      A       D ETAILED D ESCRIPTION OF DATASETS AND P REPROCESSING

848
      A.1     DATASETS FOR E VALUATION
849
850   Detailed descriptions of the eight used datasets are listed below. An overview of the datasets’ general
851   information is presented in Table 4.
852
853                           Table 4: General information of the eight datasets used in this work.
854                                           Air                     Traffic              Electricity                   Healthcare
                          Dataset    BeijingAir     ItalyAir   PeMS       Pedestrian   ETT_h1   Electricity   PhysioNet2012    PhysioNet2019
855
856            # of Total Samples      1458           774      727          3633        358         1457          3997                4927
                    # of Variables      132            13      862            1           7          370           35                  33
857     Sample Sequence Length          24            12       24            24          48           96           48                  48
858                 Time Interval       1H            1H       1H            1H         1H         15Min           1H                  1H
       Original Missing Rate (%)        1.6            0        0             0           0            0          79.3                73.9
859         Train Dataset Length        851           466      455           955        212          851          2557                3152
860    Validation Dataset Length        304           154      122           239         75          304           640                 789
             Test Dataset Length        303           154      150          2439         71          302           800                 986
861
862
      A.1.1     A IR Q UALITY DATASETS
863
864            • BeijingAir1 (Zhang et al., 2017): BeijingAir dataset includes hourly air pollutants data from 12
865              nationally-controlled air-quality monitoring sites from March 2013 to February 2017. The dataset
866              contains 11 continuous variables at multiple sites in Beijing. We aggregate these variables from all
867              sites together so this dataset has 12*11=132 features.
868            • ItalyAir2 (De Vito et al., 2008): ItalyAir dataset contains 9358 instances of hourly averaged
869              responses of 5 metal oxides from chemical sensors, along with hourly averaged concentrations of 7
870              pollutants from certified analyzers, from March 2004 to February 2005.
871
872
      A.1.2     T RAFFIC DATASETS
873            • PeMS3 : The PeMS is a collection of hourly data from the California Department of Transportation,
874              which describes the road occupancy rates measured by different sensors on San Francisco Bay area
875              freeways.
876            • Pedestrian4 (Tang et al., 2020): The City of Melbourne, Australia has developed an automated
877              pedestrian counting system to better understand pedestrian activity within the municipality, The data
878              of a specific region is from the whole year of 2017.
879
880   A.1.3     E LECTRICITY DATASETS
881            • Electricity5 : The Electricity Load Diagrams dataset contains hourly electricity consumption (kWh)
882              for 370 clients over the period from January 2011 to December 2014.
883
               • ETT6 (Zhou et al., 2021a): The ETT dataset, collected from power transformers, includes prepro-
884
                 cessed data on power load and oil temperature from July 2016 to July 2018. In the experiments, we
885              use ETT_h1 included in ETT.

          https://archive.ics.uci.edu/dataset/501/beijing+multi+site+air+quality+data
887       2
          https://archive.ics.uci.edu/dataset/360/air+quality
888     3
          https://PeMS.dot.ca.gov
889     4
          https://www.timeseriesclassification.com/description.php?Dataset=
890   MelbournePedestrian

          https://archive.ics.uci.edu/dataset/321/electricityloaddiagrams20112014
        6

      A.1.4      H EALTHCARE DATASETS
894
895             • PhysioNet20127 (Silva et al., 2012): The PhysioNet/Computing in Cardiology Challenge 2012
896               dataset includes 12,000 ICU patient records from the MIMIC II Clinical database, version 2.6 (Saeed
897               et al., 2011), focusing on patient-specific prediction of in-hospital mortality using data from the first
898
                  48 hours of ICU admission. Not all variables are available in all cases, hence about 80% values are
                  missing in this dataset. The whole dataset has three subsets, and we only use the subset A in our
899
                  experiments.
900
901             • PhysioNet20198 (Reyna et al., 2020): The PhysioNet Challenge 2019 dataset includes clinical data
902               from ICU patients across three hospitals, with a total of 40,336 patient records and 40 clinical and
903               physiological variables for each patient. Note that this dataset has two subsets, and we only use the
904
                  subset A in our setting.
905
906   A.2      DATASETS P REPROCESSING D ETAILS
907
908   BeijingAir, ItalyAir, PeMS, Electricity, and ETT_h1 are all long-time series continuously collected from
909   certain sources. Therefore, to generate them into the train, validation, and test sets and to avoid data leakage,
910
      we should first split them according to the time period. In BeijingAir, the first 28 months (2013/03 - 2015/06)
      of data are taken for training, the following 10 months (2015/07 - 2016/04) are for validation, and the left
911
      10 months (2016/05 - 2017/02) are for test. In PeMS, the training set tasks the first 15 months (2016/07 -
912
      2017/09) of data, and the validation set and test set take the following 4 months (2017/10 - 2018/01) and 6
913   months (2018/02 - 2018/07) respectively. Electricity uses the first 10 months (2011/01 - 2011/10) as the test
914   set, the following 10 months (2011/11 - 2012/08) for validation, and the last 28 months (2012/09 - 2014/12)
915   for training. The training, validation, and test sets of ETT_h1 separately take the first 14 months (2016/07 -
916   2017/08), the following 5 months (2017/09 - 2018/01), and the last 5 months (2018/02 - 2018/06). ItalyAir is
917   split into 60%, 20%, and 20% for training, validation, and test. The sliding window function is applied to
918   these five datasets to produce data samples. The window size of ItalyAir is 12, and 24 for BeijingAir and
919   PeMS, 48 for ETT_h1, and 96 for Electricity. The sliding length is kept the same as the window size to
920   guarantee there is no overlap between generated samples.
921   Dataset Pedestrian is offered with the split training set and test set, hence we separate 20% from the training
922   set to form the validation set. Data samples in PhysioNet2012 share the same length, i.e. 48 steps. While
923   samples in PhysioNet2019 have different lengths, hence we only keep samples with lengths larger than
924   48 and truncate the excess part to ensure samples all have 48 steps as well. For both PhysioNet2012 and
925   PhysioNet2019, samples are firstly split into the training set and the test set according to 80% and 20%, then
926   20% of samples are taken from the training set as the validation set.
927   Note that standardization is applied in the preprocessing of all datasets.
928

933             • iTransformer (Liu et al., 2023b): iTransformer repurposes the Transformer architecture by applying
934               attention and feed-forward networks to inverted dimensions, embedding time points into variate
935               tokens to better capture multivariate correlations and nonlinear representations, achieving state-of-
936               the-art performance in time series forecasting.

966
967         • SAITS (Du et al., 2023): SAITS (Self-Attention-based Imputation for Time Series) is a self-
968           attention imputation transformer with a weighted combination of two diagonally-masked self-
969           attention (DMSA) blocks. It is designed to handle missing data in time series, ensuring robust and
970           accurate data imputation.
971         • Nonstationary (Liu et al., 2022c): Nonstationary (short for Nonstationary Transformer) addresses
972           the challenge of non-stationarity in time series sequences by incorporating adaptive components that
973           adjust to varying statistical properties over time.
974
            • ETSformer (Woo et al., 2022): ETSformer integrates exponential smoothing methods with Trans-
975
              former models, aiming to provide accurate time series forecasting by combining statistical and deep
976
              learning approaches.
977
978         • PatchTST (Nie et al., 2023): PatchTST uses a patching strategy combined with a Transformer
979
              architecture to enhance the time series forecasting task by capturing both local and global patterns
              effectively.
980
981         • Crossformer (Zhang & Yan, 2022): Crossformer leverages cross-dimensional attention to model
982           intricate dependencies within multivariate time series data, achieving good performance in complex
983           forecasting application scenarios.
984         • Informer (Zhou et al., 2021b): Informer enhances efficiency in long-time series forecasting by
985           employing a self-attention distillation mechanism, which reduces redundant information while
986           maintaining forecasting accuracy.

       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


987
             • Autoformer (Wu et al., 2021): Autoformer introduces a novel decomposition architecture with an
988
               auto-correlation mechanism, effectively capturing both seasonal and trend patterns in time series
989            forecasting tasks.
990
991
             • Pyraformer (Liu et al., 2021): Pyraformer is designed for long-term time series forecasting,
               utilizing a pyramid attention structure that efficiently captures temporal dependencies at multiple
992
               scales.
993
994          • Transformer (Vaswani et al., 2017): Transformer introduces the self-attention mechanism, which
995            enables the processing of sequential data by attending to different positions within the sequence
996
               simultaneously, leading to significant advancements in natural language processing and time series
               fields.
997
998
999    B.2   RNN- BASED M ODELS
1000         • BRITS (Cao et al., 2018): BRITS (Bidirectional Recurrent Imputation for Time Series) employs
1001           a bidirectional recurrent imputation strategy to handle missing values in time series, improving
1002           forecasting accuracy through iterative refinement.
1003
             • MRNN (Yoon et al., 2018): The Multi-directional Recurrent Neural Network (MRNN) is de-
1004           signed for estimating missing values in spatiotemporal data and time series by leveraging temporal
1005           dependencies and multi-directional information sequence.
1006
             • GRUD (Che et al., 2018b): GRUD (Gated Recurrent Unit for Decay) enhances the Gated Recurrent
1007
               Unit (GRU) by incorporating decay mechanisms that model the impact of missing values over time,
1008
               offering robust time series analysis.
1009
1010
1011
       B.3   CNN- BASED M ODELS
1012         • TimesNet (Wu et al., 2023): TimesNet is designed to efficiently model temporal patterns in time
1013           series data by incorporating multi-scale temporal convolutions and attention mechanisms. It can be
1014           used for short- and long-term forecasting, imputation, classification, and anomaly detection tasks.
1015         • MICN (Wang et al., 2022): MICN (Multi-scale Inception Convolutional Network) is a convolutional
1016           network architecture that captures multi-scale temporal features and combines local and global
1017           context for more accurate time series forecasting.
1018
             • SCINet (Liu et al., 2022a): SCINet introduces a novel recursive downsample-convolve-interact
1019           architecture for time series forecasting, leveraging multiple convolutional filters to extract and
1020           aggregate valuable temporal features from downsampled sub-sequences, improving forecasting
1021           accuracy over existing models.
1022
1023
       B.4   GNN- BASED M ODELS
1024
1025         • StemGNN (Cao et al., 2020): StemGNN (Spectral Temporal Graph Neural Network) introduces
1026           an approach for time series forecasting by jointly capturing inter-series correlations and temporal
1027
               dependencies in the spectral domain using Graph Fourier Transform (GFT) and Discrete Fourier
               Transform (DFT), eliminating the need for pre-defined priors.
1028
1029
1030   B.5   MLP- BASED M ODELS
1031         • FreTS (Yi et al., 2024): FreTS uses MLPs in the frequency domain for time series forecasting,
1032           leveraging global view and energy compaction properties to enhance forecasting performance by
1033           transforming time-domain signals and learning frequency components.

                                                      22
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1034
             • Koopa (Liu et al., 2024): Koopa introduces a novel Koopman forecaster that disentangles time-
1035
               variant and time-invariant components using Fourier Filter and employs Koopman operators for
1036           linear dynamics portrayal, achieving end-to-end forecasting with significant improvements in training
1037           time and memory efficiency.
1038
1039         • DLinear (Zeng et al., 2023): DLinear introduces LTSF-Linear, a set of simple one-layer linear
1040           models, which outperform complex Transformer-based models in long-term time series forecast-
1041           ing by effectively preserving temporal information that Transformers inherently lose due to their
1042           permutation-invariant self-attention mechanism.
1043
             • FiLM (Zhou et al., 2022): FiLM (Frequency improved Legendre Memory) introduces a novel
1044
               approach by using Legendre Polynomials for historical information approximation, Fourier projec-
1045
               tion for noise reduction, and a low-rank approximation for computational efficiency, resulting in
1046           significant improvements in long-term forecasting accuracy.
1047
1048
1049   B.6   G ENERATIVE M ODELS
1050
             • CSDI (Tashiro et al., 2021): CSDI (Conditional Score-based Diffusion Imputation) leverages
1051           conditional score-based diffusion models for accurate imputation and generation of missing values
1052           in time series applications.
1053
1054         • US-GAN (Miao et al., 2021a): US-GAN integrates a classifier in a semi-supervised generative
1055           adversarial network to enhance its imputation of missing values in multivariate time series, leveraging
1056           both observed data and label information. Also, it introduces a temporal matrix to improve the
1057           discriminator’s ability to differentiate between observed and imputed components.
1058
             • GP-VAE (Fortuin et al., 2020): GP-VAE (Gaussian Process Variational Autoencoder) proposes
1059
               a novel deep sequential latent variable model that combines VAE with a structured variational
1060
               approximation to achieve non-linear dimensionality reduction and imputation, providing interpretable
1061           uncertainty estimates and improved imputation smoothness.
1062
1063
1064   B.7   T RADITIONAL M ETHODS
1065
             • Mean: The mean imputation method fills in missing values by calculating the mean (average) of the
1066
               available values in the time series. This method is simple and quick to implement but may not be
1067           suitable for data with trends or seasonality, as it does not consider the temporal structure of the data.
1068
1069         • Median: Median imputation replaces missing values with the median value of the observed data
1070           points. The median is the middle value when the data points are ordered, making this method robust
1071           to outliers. It is particularly useful for skewed distributions or data with significant outliers.
1072
1073         • LOCF: LOCF (Last Observation Carried Forward) fills in missing values by carrying forward the
1074
               last observed value. This method assumes that the value remains constant until the next observation
               is recorded. LOCF is straightforward and works well for short gaps in data, but it can introduce bias
1075
               if the missing data spans a long period or if the data has a trend.
1076
1077         • Linear: Linear interpolation fills in missing values by connecting the last observed value before the
1078           missing data and the first observed value after the missing data with a straight line. This method
1079           assumes a linear trend between the two points and is useful for data with a relatively stable and linear
1080           pattern. However, it may not perform well with data that exhibit non-linear trends or seasonality.

                                                         23
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1081
       C                                  D ETAILS IN TSI-B ENCH
1082
1083
       C.1                                    M ISSINGNESS
1084
1085   The concept of missingness can be understood through two fundamental aspects: missing mechanisms and
1086   missing patterns. Missing mechanisms describe the underlying processes that lead to missing values, while
1087   missing patterns define how these missing values manifest within the dataset.
1088
1089   Missing Mechanisms The seminal taxonomy introduced by (Rubin, 1976) categorizes missing data mecha-
1090   nisms into three distinct types: Missing Completely At Random (MCAR), Missing At Random (MAR), and
1091   Missing Not At Random (MNAR). These classifications hinge on the conditional probability distribution
1092   p(M|X̃, ϕ), where ϕ parameterizes the stochastic mapping from the dataset X̃ to the mask indicator M.
1093   Specifically:
1094
1095                                          • MCAR implies that the probability of data being missing is independent of observations and
1096                                            missingness. This can be expressed as: p(M|X̃, ϕ) = p(M|ϕ),
1097
                                              • MAR suggests that the missingness depends solely on observations p(M|X̃, ϕ) = p(M|X, ϕ),
1098
1099                                          • MNAR indicates that the missingness is related to the unobserved data and potentially the observed
1100                                            data as well:p(M|X̃, ϕ) = p(M|X̃, ϕ).
1101
1102   Missing Patterns Despite the clarity the above taxonomy provides, it often falls short in capturing the
1103   complexity of missing data scenarios in modern datasets (Mitra et al., 2023). This limitation has driven the
1104   development of new frameworks (Mitra et al., 2023; Cini et al., 2022; Khayati et al., 2020) that better align
1105   with diverse real-world data.
1106   To effectively analyze missingness in time series data, we employ three distinct patterns of missing data:
1107   point, subsequence, and block. These patterns are specifically chosen to represent typical missing data
1108   scenarios in time series analysis, providing a robust framework for evaluating different imputation strategies.
1109   The missingness construction functions are directly available in the Python library PyGrinder9 . The
1110   visualization of them is plotted in figures 6(a), 6(b), and 6(c) for vivid illustration.
1111

1132   Note that errors are only calculated for values indicated by the mask in the input.

1144
1145   C.3   H YPER - PARAMETER O PTIMIZATION
1146
1147   The performance of deep-learning models highly depends on the settings of hyper-parameters. To make
1148
       fair comparisons across imputation methods and draw impartial conclusions, all neural network-based
       algorithms in TSI-Bench get their hyper-parameters optimized by PyPOTS and Microsoft NNI (Neural
1149
       Network Intelligence). For each algorithm, we run at least 100 trials (i.e. tune it with 100 groups of hyper-
1150
       parameters) and use the group of the best to produce the formal results. The HPO configuration files and the
1151   fixed model hyper-parameters are available in the supplementary material.
1152
1153
       C.4   D OWNSTREAM TASK D ESIGN
1154
1155   To further discuss the benefits that imputation can bring to downstream analysis, experiments are designed
1156   and conducted on three common downstream tasks (classification, regression, and forecasting) to evaluate
1157   the imputation quality across algorithms. For these three tasks, missing values in the selected datasets are
1158   imputed by different algorithms. Then XGBoost, LSTM, and Transformer perform downstream analysis
1159   tasks on the imputed datasets with fixed hyper-parameters. XGBoost, LSTM, and Transformer are selected
1160   because they are respectively representative models for classical machine learning approaches, traditional
1161
       RNN methods, and emerging attention algorithms. Besides, thanks to XGBoost it can handle missing values
       by default. It allows us to compare XGBoost performance results with and without imputation to observe
1162
       whether imputation can help.
1163
1164
       Classification PhysioNet2012 and Pedestrian are chosen for the classification task because every sample
1165
       has a classification label. In PhysioNet2012, each sample has a label indicating if the patient is deceased in
1166   ICU, making it an unbalanced binary classification dataset with 13.9% positive samples. Sample classes in
1167   Pedestrian correspond to ten locations of sensor placement.
1168
1169   Regression ETT_h1 and PeMS both have a target feature that makes them suitable for the regression and
1170   forecasting task. In the regression task, except for the target feature, the imputed data with other features are
1171   fed into the downstream algorithms to produce the regression results of the target feature.
1172
1173   Forecasting For both ETT_h1 and PeMS datasets, we input the imputed data samples without their target
1174   features and the last five steps, then let the downstream models forecast the future five steps of the target

                                                         25
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1175
       feature. Note that, different from the above regression task that inputs the full-length samples and outputs
1176
       corresponding regression results, the forecasting task holds out the last five steps of the samples as ground
1177   truth to evaluate the forecasting performance, i.e. its input only contains (sample length - 5) steps.
1178
1179   As additional experiments, the forecasting downstream task is not mentioned in the main body of this paper,
1180
       but its experimental results are presented in Appendix D.
1181
1182

       and proposes such an adaptation paradigm that has been demonstrated
1188
       to be feasible in this work’s extensive experiments.

1332   D.4    50% S UBSEQUENCE M ISSING
1333
1334   The details of the preprocessed datasets in this setting are shown as Table 9, and table 14 shows the imputation
1335   results with 50% subsequence missing. It could be observed that the imputation error is generally higher
1336   than that with 50% point missing. In this condition, the missing values in the subsequence cannot be easily
1337   estimated from their adjacent observed data.

1342
       50% block missing. This missing pattern includes missing data across multiple dimensions at the same time
       points and consecutive missing data at multiple time points, thus presenting challenges for imputation.

1350
1351
       D.7    V ISUALIZATION OF T IME I NTERVALS
1352
1353   We conducted a detailed statistical analysis and visualization of the time intervals in the PhysioNet2012
1354   dataset, as shown in Figures 11. It is crucial to emphasize that the uneven time intervals in medical data
1355   represent a significant and common issue. As the figure illustrates, features such as heart rate (HR) and
1356   diastolic arterial blood pressure (DiasABP) tend to have shorter time intervals, while features like white blood
1357   cell count (WBC) and sodium levels (Na) have much longer intervals. This discrepancy arises from the nature
1358   of these measurements—vital signs like heart rate can be continuously monitored by instruments, whereas
1359   blood tests are inherently spaced out due to the invasive nature of the procedures.
1360   Furthermore, different types of features, such as continuous variables (e.g., HR, DiasABP) versus categorical
1361   variables (e.g., Glasgow Coma Scale [GCS]), exhibit significant variability in their measurement intervals.
1362   Existing methods like BRITS and GRU-D attempt to address this by incorporating decay mechanisms,

1445
1446   Table 21, 22 and 23 show the forecasting performance without or with imputation under different missing
1447   patterns and show the same phenomenon when regression is the downstream task, that is, using advanced

1449   methods like Mean may not have a positive impact on forecasting performance.

1481                               Figure 11: Density distribution of time intervals across features in PhysioNet2012 dataset.
1482
1483   HPO experiments. For 24 neural networks, we run 100 HPO trials for each of them on each dataset, with
1484   24 ∗ 100 ∗ 8 = 19, 200 experiments.
1485
1486
       Imputation experiments. For 24 neural network models, we run 5 rounds on 8 datasets with 10% point
       missing, 6 datasets with 50% point missing, 6 datasets with 90% point missing, 6 datasets with 50%
1487
       subsequence missing, and 5 datasets with 50% block missing, with 24 ∗ 5 ∗ (8 + 6 + 6 + 6 + 5) = 3, 720
1488
       experiments. We also conduct 4 ∗ (8 + 6 + 6 + 6 + 5) = 124 experiments for 4 naive imputation methods.
1489
1490   Downstream experiments. Experiments on the downstream tasks are performed in 5 rounds with 4 algorithms
1491   on each dataset imputed by 28 methods. The classification task is on PhysioNet2012 with 10% point missing
1492
       in the evaluation sets and Pedestrian with 10%, 50%, and 90% point missing, and 50% subsequence missing.
       The regression and forecasting tasks are on ETT_h1 and PeMS with 10% and 50% points missing, 50%
1493
       subsequence missing and block missing. So the total number of downstream tasks is 5 ∗ 4 ∗ 28 ∗ (1 + 4) +
1494
       5 ∗ 4 ∗ 28 ∗ (4 + 4) + 5 ∗ 4 ∗ 28 ∗ (4 + 4) = 11, 760 experiments.
